  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2623f8e7-f747-4765-9825-494e12b948ee'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer11798734260851455724">Daniel Weir used to be a famous - not to say infamous - rock star. Maybe still is.   <span id="freeText11798734260851455724" style="display:none">Daniel Weir used to be a famous - not to say infamous - rock star. Maybe still is. At thirty-one he has been both a brilliant failure and a dull success. He's made a lot of mistakes that have paid off and a lot of smart moves he'll regret forever (however long that turns out to be). Daniel Weir has gone from rags to riches and back, and managed to hold onto them both, though not much else. His friends all seem to be dead, fed up with him or just disgusted - and who can blame them? And now Daniel Weir is all alone. As he contemplates his life, Daniel realises he only has two problems: the past and the future. He knows how bad the past has been. But the future - well, the future is something else.</span>

  <span id="freeText13862965195870559713" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2020/07/its-wonderful-life-espedair-street-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />It's a Wonderful Life: "Espedair Street" by Iain Banks<br /><br /><br />In the 90s I was shagging left and right but after a while I wanted to spend some time on Christmas without girl distractions because of my exams in January. <div id="review_2850071187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2850071187" />
    <a title="Paltia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7271273-paltia"><img alt="Paltia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545851814p2/7271273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2850071187?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paltia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paltia" href="/user/show/7271273-paltia">Paltia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349139258" href="/book/show/17465169-espedair-street">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2850071187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11482250271554869575">“Everything seems to take about the right amount of time at the time, but later...Jeez, where did it all go? You look back, and sometimes you think, Did I really do all that? And other times you think. Is that all there is? Is that all I managed to get done? We are never satisfied. Don’t even know the meaning of the word.”<br/>Daniel Wier has a story to tell. A story of his search for satisfaction. A writer of songs, a bass player and a man who nearly loses his way as a member of the successful band,</span>
  <span id="freeText11482250271554869575" style="display:none">“Everything seems to take about the right amount of time at the time, but later...Jeez, where did it all go? You look back, and sometimes you think, Did I really do all that? And other times you think. Is that all there is? Is that all I managed to get done? We are never satisfied. Don’t even know the meaning of the word.”<br />Daniel Wier has a story to tell. A story of his search for satisfaction. A writer of songs, a bass player and a man who nearly loses his way as a member of the successful band, Frozen Gold. Despite, or due to his tendencies towards self doubt and pessimism, he knows how to make music. He surprises himself once the words that previously existed only in his head go live particularly with Christine singing his words. His music takes the band and fans to places of ecstasy and abandonment. On his journey he is saddled with the hefty baggage of Catholic guilt, an absent father in prison, childhood trauma, and what he describes as a less than pleasing countenance. Is he confused? He is. Better confused than bored, miserable and serious. His confusion teaches him to think for himself which at times is in conflict with acting the rock star. He feels he has a duty to his public. He must act the part. In these moments he can almost convince himself that nothing is really all that important, so why not party. These irresponsible moments are the most comic. But, just when you think you know this character he upends the chaos and reveals his other sides. He is gentle, humble, longing for closeness and genuinely caring for and about others. Rarely self absorbed or self righteous he strives for a balance amidst the decadence. In his most self reflective moments he reminds us that life is a dance and if you aren’t dancing but holding fast to rigid frameworks you might consider allowing the tide of music to sweep you away. There is immense loss in his story. These losses are so vast they nearly take him along. Fortunate enough to see another sunrise he acts on his final decision of the story. A decision so important it’s as if he knew all along he would arrive exactly where he does.<br />This is an altogether gratifying story which offers insight into survival - how to survive the random and unexpected events life throws at us. Daniel finds his strength and satisfaction through surviving and this is what makes him a real star.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11482250271554869575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78603816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15199130552006923319">I’ve always been impressed by Iain (M.) Banks range. <br/><br/>Whether he’s writing about an hermaphroditic serial killer and his/her mechanical wasp oracle, a man in a coma living a second life on a vast hyperreal bridge of the mind, a bored gamer compelled by artificial intelligences to play the ultimate game on a distant planet, or a brilliant woman whose place in an omnipotent corporation takes her to a kingdom in the Himalayas, Banks always maintains his artistry and deliberate social relevance witho</span>
  <span id="freeText15199130552006923319" style="display:none">I’ve always been impressed by Iain (M.) Banks range. <br /><br />Whether he’s writing about an hermaphroditic serial killer and his/her mechanical wasp oracle, a man in a coma living a second life on a vast hyperreal bridge of the mind, a bored gamer compelled by artificial intelligences to play the ultimate game on a distant planet, or a brilliant woman whose place in an omnipotent corporation takes her to a kingdom in the Himalayas, Banks always maintains his artistry and deliberate social relevance without compromising entertainment. <br /><br />Despite Banks’ excellence, however, his books tend to be too edgy for mainstream audiences. My biggest challenge has been finding the right work to pass on to my friends, to ease them into the mind of Banks, to prepare them for his more challenging works and the intellectual challenges that often lie in wait. That novel is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68772.Espedair_Street" title="Espedair Street by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Espedair Street</a>. <br /><br />It isn’t just close to mainstream, it <u>is</u> mainstream. <br /><br />It tells the story of Daniel Weir, ex-bass player and musical genius behind the 70s’ supergroup Frozen Gold. Daniel, also known as Weird, is a bit depressed when the novel opens, depressed enough to consider suicide, and he relates his life story to us so that we understand why he’s feeling down but decided to hang around. He talks about missed opportunities, wild successes, the deaths of people he loved (which were marginally his fault), the people he let slip away, the talent he leaves fallow, sex, drugs and even some Rock and Roll. And when Weird’s told us everything he needs to tell us, after he’s made us love him without pity, he goes off and finds happiness. <br /><br />Yep, Iain Banks wrote a happy ending. He wrote a book that was made for the screen (and the fact that it hasn’t been adapted is criminal). He wrote a book whose primary purpose seems to be escape, although it still retains elements of Banks’ conscience and politics. He wrote a book that even the most genre-phobic reader would be thrilled to read. <br /><br />And it is proof positive that there is nothing that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7628.Iain_M__Banks" title="Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Iain Banks</a> can’t write. What wouldn’t I give for a gram of his range. <br /><br />Aside -- (over the course of Espedair Street I couldn’t get this thought out of my head: Banks “Weird” tale is a Nick Horby novel without the smug cheek and slacker superiority. I wonder how Hornby fans would like Espedair Street. I’m betting they would like it very much.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15199130552006923319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101034513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101034513" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101034513?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/170420-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170420-laura?shelf=mt-bookpile-2010">mt-bookpile-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170420-laura?shelf=old-reads">old-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101034513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15314939038903247623">First of all, this is NOT a book by "Iain M. Banks" - it's by Iain Banks (in other words, it's fiction, not science-fiction). Second, why he's so difficult to find in the US I'll never know. Every book I've read is just good, solid fiction writing, equal to Julian Barnes, Robertson Davies and many other top echelon writers.<br/><br/>Espedair Street is a real street, but it barely figures in this tale of Wierd, the stage name for one Daniel Weir (in school he was Weir, D. - get it?). He's the lyricist and </span>
  <span id="freeText15314939038903247623" style="display:none">First of all, this is NOT a book by "Iain M. Banks" - it's by Iain Banks (in other words, it's fiction, not science-fiction). Second, why he's so difficult to find in the US I'll never know. Every book I've read is just good, solid fiction writing, equal to Julian Barnes, Robertson Davies and many other top echelon writers.<br /><br />Espedair Street is a real street, but it barely figures in this tale of Wierd, the stage name for one Daniel Weir (in school he was Weir, D. - get it?). He's the lyricist and bassist for Frozen Gold, a hugely successful rock band in the 70s-80s until their lead guitarist dies. Told partly in flashbacks of his days with FG and partly in his present guise as Jimmy Hay, caretaker of St. Jute's, the folly that Weird bought, drinking his way through life and occasionally creating a jingle or a movie score. His current friends don't know who he is, or that he's stinking rich; his former friends/fans think he's dead (or perhaps living in the Caymans). It all comes abruptly to an end when he realizes that his brilliant ideas have killed (inadvertently) two of his bandmates. So, what was that about a future?<br /><br />The characters feel real - even though you know this is fiction, you could plausibly meet someone just like them. The writing is crisp, not the bloated stories that often appear today because somehow bigger = better.<br /><br />Seriously, if you don't know Banks' non-scifi work, find one of his works of fiction and read it, now. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15314939038903247623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1756053333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1756053333" />
    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1887905-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510181931p2/1887905.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1756053333?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/1887905-kevin">Kevin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349139258" href="/book/show/17465169-espedair-street">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1887905-kevin?shelf=contemporary-fiction-modern-ish">contemporary-fiction-modern-ish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1756053333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17277902268609318143">After learning about the untimely passing of Iain Banks a few years ago, I made it a task that I would finish reading his quite substantial body of work, both his contemporary novels (of which I had read several over the years, starting with the infamous Wasp Factory back in the very early 1990's) and his Science Fiction based ones, written under Iain M. Banks. In fact I had started on the Culture novels before I learned he was dying. He was a great writer, his contemporary, more 'mainstream' bo</span>
  <span id="freeText17277902268609318143" style="display:none">After learning about the untimely passing of Iain Banks a few years ago, I made it a task that I would finish reading his quite substantial body of work, both his contemporary novels (of which I had read several over the years, starting with the infamous Wasp Factory back in the very early 1990's) and his Science Fiction based ones, written under Iain M. Banks. In fact I had started on the Culture novels before I learned he was dying. He was a great writer, his contemporary, more 'mainstream' books all usually contain some dry humour and wit, along with quite bizarre plots, protagonists and a usual 'twist'. Espedair Street is no exception, but it is more of a straightforward (well...maybe not <i>quite</i> so) tale, lacking the twists compared to say, The Crow Road for example.<br /><br />It deals with a working class Glaswegian youth called Daniel Weir (nicknamed 'Weird'), who has high ambitions of becoming a rock star, which, through incredible luck succeeds in doing throughout the 1970s with a progressive rock band called 'Frozen Gold'. The band he is in (being the main songwriter and bass player) soon become a stadium rock outfit, making millions, and the book traverses all the typical lifestyle and tragedy that those big mega-groups of the mid '70s are infamous for; sex, drugs n' rock n'roll - in a nutshell. I was trying to place, with my own knowledge of prog-rock banks of the 1970's (which is not inconsiderable) who possibly Frozen Gold could have been based on, but found it hard. Perhaps the whole tale is really a send-up of the excesses of the big bands of the '70's, a wry look at what fame does to people and how manipulated by record companies they were (and probably still are to an extent). Perhaps Frozen Gold could have been as much Led Zeppelin as the Rolling Stones as Fleetwood Mac, maybe. Whatever, if there is a message here in Espedair Street, it is what fame and excessive amounts of money can do to you. Daniel Weir however appears slightly more balanced with it all compared to some of the band - perhaps coming from a real deprived working-class area of Glasgow keeps his mind in check, his feet on the ground. Iain Banks' dry humour is here, but there is some sadness throughout the book as well. Also, there is not that much of twist here that his novels are famed for, as I mentioned above, and the book ends happily and positively for the protagonist (returning to his roots after experiencing over a decade as a famous Rock Star, finding his old girlfriend). A good tale, but still not quite as good as The Bridge (which, of the ones I have read, I consider his best - but that is a different format anyway and deals with a much more deeper theme than Espedair Street) and a few others. <br /><br />EDIT: --<br />Espedair Street...is located in Paisley, Glasgow, and Daniel Weir, according to Iain Banks, was loosely based on Fish, the ex-Marillion singer/solo artist. And, from reading the Wikipedia page - Frozen Gold was again, loosely based on Pink Floyd AND Fleetwood Mac (so I got part of the influence correct). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17277902268609318143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2859422538" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2859422538" />
    <a title="Marcus Markou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/98587528-marcus-markou"><img alt="Marcus Markou" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2859422538?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marcus Markou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marcus Markou" href="/user/show/98587528-marcus-markou">Marcus Markou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2859422538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12056759239442156091">I bought this having wandered into a book shop aged 16, in the Summer of 1987. I was taken by its strange black and white lumpy cover. We were an immediate match. It was my Catcher in the Rye and Iain Banks was my hero. I have subsequently re-read it and it takes me back to being 16 and all those clumsy feelings about wondering who I would be when I was older and how it would all turn out still reverberate like a favourite song. I have no idea whether this is a good book or not - objectively spe</span>
  <span id="freeText12056759239442156091" style="display:none">I bought this having wandered into a book shop aged 16, in the Summer of 1987. I was taken by its strange black and white lumpy cover. We were an immediate match. It was my Catcher in the Rye and Iain Banks was my hero. I have subsequently re-read it and it takes me back to being 16 and all those clumsy feelings about wondering who I would be when I was older and how it would all turn out still reverberate like a favourite song. I have no idea whether this is a good book or not - objectively speaking. But it is my favourite book. Years later, whilst working in London, I noticed that Iain Banks was signing a new Sci Fi book as Iain M Banks. It was my lunch hour. I just started my first job out of University. Again, it was Summer and I was wondering who I would be when I was older and how it would all turn out. I queued up with every Espedair Street the shop was selling - five copies. I had a good school friend who also loved this book. I didn't know what I would do with the rest of them but I wanted his signature on them all. "I hope you don't mind," I nervously asked, when I got to the small desk in the book shop where Mr Banks was signing Sci Fi. "As long as it's not a f**king Geoffrey Archer novel, I will sign it," he said laughing. The queue behind me also laughed. It is a precious memory. Many years later when he died, I cried. Last night I took my last signed copy of Espedair Street from my bookshelf (over the years I'd given all the others away - to lovers and friends) and gave it to my son. He is 14 and for some reason he has found himself playing base at lunchtimes at school and he is starting to wonder who he will be when he's older and how it will all turn out. We both Googled to see whether anyone had made a movie of this book. I am a filmmaker. But we came across this review site. I don't do reviews. Any reviews. For anything. But for this book, I made an exception. Thank you Iain. It's a really special book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12056759239442156091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_794908434" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794908434" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2544254-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/794908434?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/2544254-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer794908434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4453280538309679662">At first I was wondering what all the fuss was about, why some friends thought this was Banks' masterpiece. It seemed a fairly simple tale of a rock star looking back [from the dizzy heights of his early thirties!] on his life so far with the way his band was formed and how he felt about it now. But gradually I realised there was more to it than that, the book's structure more complex and the layers within the plot more intriguing. As Danny's life was revealed, so I became more fascinated with t</span>
  <span id="freeText4453280538309679662" style="display:none">At first I was wondering what all the fuss was about, why some friends thought this was Banks' masterpiece. It seemed a fairly simple tale of a rock star looking back [from the dizzy heights of his early thirties!] on his life so far with the way his band was formed and how he felt about it now. But gradually I realised there was more to it than that, the book's structure more complex and the layers within the plot more intriguing. As Danny's life was revealed, so I became more fascinated with the characters he knew and the life-style he led then and now. I appreciated the hints Banks left occasionally about tragedies and dramatic events yet to be told ... I was hooked. I wanted to know more about the band, Frozen Gold, and the lad himself. Was he based on Mick Fleetwood [physically and past habits!], Fish from Marillion [as Banks once claimed] or another rock-star of the 1970/80s?<br /><br />It's difficult to say too much about what happened later as this would leave spoilers but suffice it to say that I was drawn into the world of Weird/Danny/Jim, especially all the characters from McCann and Wee Tommy in the present to Christine and Inez in the past. Each was believable and well drawn. The settings too were clear, from Ferguslie Park in Paisley to the tours and back to Clydeside.<br /><br />I have some hesitations about the ending ... perhaps too neat, too happy ... but overall an excellent book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4453280538309679662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7999752590095219861">Another great book from Banks.   <span id="freeText7999752590095219861" style="display:none">Another great book from Banks. I've read a few Biography/auto-biography type books about rock bands; including the Stones, The Beatles, Fleetwood Mac and David Bowie. This had a lot of the same elements to it, but with an honesty that the others can't match. <br /><br />I want to quote a little piece from near the end of the book. To me, this portion of a long, run-on sentence, is the entire point of what Banks was trying to say with Espedair Street.<br /><br />"...it felt like faith, like revelation: that things went on, that life ground on regardless, and mindless, and produced pain and pleasure and hope and fear and joy and dispair, and you were lucky and sometimes you weren't, and sometimes you could plan your way ahead and that would be the right thing to have done, but other times all you could do was forget about plans and just be ready to react, and sometimes the obvious was true and sometimes it wasn't, and sometimes experience helped but not always, and it was all luck, fate, in the end; you lived, and you waited to see what happened, and you would rarely ever be sure that what you had done was really the right thing or the wrong thing, because things can always be better, and things can always be worse." </span>

<div id="review_1813370767" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1813370767" />
    <a title="Owain Lewis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5968963-owain-lewis"><img alt="Owain Lewis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334514245p2/5968963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1813370767?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Owain Lewis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Owain Lewis" href="/user/show/5968963-owain-lewis">Owain Lewis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1813370767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11083216929019855788">No, sorry, I just didn't buy it. I think setting up his narrator as a former big name in the world of late sixties/early seventies rock, operating in the same arena as Zepp and Hendrix, was a bad move. We can read biographies/autobiographies of actual rock mega stars and their debauched shenanigans so a fictional narrative is never going to cut it for me. I rather read a story about the ones that didn't make it big. That would be much more believable.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2610585535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2610585535" />
    <a title="Kate Stone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64264759-kate-stone"><img alt="Kate Stone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496963460p2/64264759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2610585535?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate Stone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate Stone" href="/user/show/64264759-kate-stone">Kate Stone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20519242-espedair-street">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2610585535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7135268025652162017">Confession: I’ve never read any of Iain Banks’ work, with or without the middle initial. I’ll be correcting that now that I’ve had my first taste. I’m also a hobbyist musician so the premise here was practically perfect for drawing me in. Draw me in it did, as I found every opportunity to read another few pages, another chapter, just one more revelation to help paint a more complete picture.<br/><br/>The book’s meandering narrative timeline was used as a really effective tool without the handholding I usu</span>
  <span id="freeText7135268025652162017" style="display:none">Confession: I’ve never read any of Iain Banks’ work, with or without the middle initial. I’ll be correcting that now that I’ve had my first taste. I’m also a hobbyist musician so the premise here was practically perfect for drawing me in. Draw me in it did, as I found every opportunity to read another few pages, another chapter, just one more revelation to help paint a more complete picture.<br /><br />The book’s meandering narrative timeline was used as a really effective tool without the handholding I usually associate with the device. It isn’t always clear where the passage you’re reading fits into the big picture, and yet I never found it confusing so much as engaging and intriguing. The story told has moments worthy of celebration and despair, and somehow both are filtered through the protagonist’s viewpoint in a way that adds another layer to his character study. An intriguing story well told, one that is over all too quickly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7135268025652162017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText812307011769422337" style="display:none">My reading group decided to tackle this book with Daisy Jones and the Six and they made a great pair and whilst I enjoyed the former this felt altogether more down to earth in its portrayal of a former song writer/bass player for a huge 1970's rock band coming to terms with life after fame in the 1980's particularly the events that brought an end to the band. <div id="review_388243329" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388243329" />
    <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish"><img alt="Simon Mcleish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347099463p2/7915393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388243329?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Mcleish" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish">Simon Mcleish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/15720057-espedair-street">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915393-simon-mcleish?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388243329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9129425095592334735">Originally published on my blog <a href='http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2003/09/iain-banks-espedair-street-1987.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> in September 2003.<br/><br/>Ever since I first read it, not long after it first came out, Espedair Street has been one of my favourite Iain Banks novels. It is his first more or less mainstream novel, neither experimental nor genre fiction, and every time I read it it still manages to amuse and move me.<br/><br/>Espedair Street is a novel which is pretending to be a rock star autobiography; the story of (fictional) seventies band Frozen Gold as told by bass player and song write</span>
  <span id="freeText9129425095592334735" style="display:none">Originally published on my blog <a href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2003/09/iain-banks-espedair-street-1987.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> in September 2003.<br /><br />Ever since I first read it, not long after it first came out, Espedair Street has been one of my favourite Iain Banks novels. It is his first more or less mainstream novel, neither experimental nor genre fiction, and every time I read it it still manages to amuse and move me.<br /><br />Espedair Street is a novel which is pretending to be a rock star autobiography; the story of (fictional) seventies band Frozen Gold as told by bass player and song writer Danny Weir (known as Weird). It is told using one of Banks' favourite devices, the series of flashbacks which converge to and explain the present (Danny living as a recluse, pretending to be his own caretaker in a bizarre Victorian folly in Glasgow). Espedair Street is about the emptiness that can come to fill the life of someone who has realised all his dreams on a massive scale. It's about lots of other things too - the hedonistic life of a seventies rock star (modelled on the excesses of bands like Fleetwood Mac); the things that we do that we regret and feel guilty about later, and the effects that these have on us; Glasgow and the people who live there.<br /><br />Much of the pleasure (and certainly the humour) provided by Espedair Street (named after a place in Glasgow where several important events in Danny's life occur) is derived from the accuracy with which much of it mimics "warts-and-all" rock journalism, and it has in fact been turned into a radio serial which takes precisely this form, complete with music written by Banks himself. This makes it immediately believable as a novel, the ludicrous actions of band members being no madder than many documented of real musicians.<br /><br />Espedair Street's main weakness is the sentimentality of its ending; this quality begins to creep into Banks' writing from this point onwards, making it more accessible but bringing occasional disappointment. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9129425095592334735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1262349307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262349307" />
    <a title="Benjamin Richards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24402846-benjamin-richards"><img alt="Benjamin Richards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573055985p2/24402846.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1262349307?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Richards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Richards" href="/user/show/24402846-benjamin-richards">Benjamin Richards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Patricio</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Benjamin by: </span>
      Matt
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262349307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13453995928227613313">I would have preferred to give it 4.5 stars as but 4 are too few, so 5 it is. I didn't quite know what to expect, I thought the dialogue and the narrative would be quirkier and then I saw when it was written - it surprised me. It seems to me that here in Paisley, Glasgow with its wall's held up by graffiti, Irvine Welsh could easily have drawn inspiration. The only other Iain Banks book I'd read was 'A Song of Stone' (hardback) many years ago, too many years ago, meaning too few years of me, to </span>
  <span id="freeText13453995928227613313" style="display:none">I would have preferred to give it 4.5 stars as but 4 are too few, so 5 it is. I didn't quite know what to expect, I thought the dialogue and the narrative would be quirkier and then I saw when it was written - it surprised me. It seems to me that here in Paisley, Glasgow with its wall's held up by graffiti, Irvine Welsh could easily have drawn inspiration. The only other Iain Banks book I'd read was 'A Song of Stone' (hardback) many years ago, too many years ago, meaning too few years of me, to really understand the nuances.<br /><br />I love the way he speaks of being, '... for a large part of the year so out of touch; they could start the next world war and I wouldn't know it.' - A precursor to the age of information, and a Thoreau-esque sensibility of escaping the pervasive grasp of news corporation.<br /><br />'Ladies glowed, gentlemen perspired and horses sweat' - so many hankerings for a gone by time. Whether this is in character with Daniel Weir or Banks' own longing? Nevertheless it is balanced between nostalgia and the contemporaneous.<br /><br />Lastly, I have been reading this whilst travelling through Vietnam, Cambodia and Laos. In no place have I visited where the sentiments of 'the only thinking animal on the goddamn planet, and what do we spend most of our time trying not to do?' Banks used the whole page to castigate the button pushers, the 'it's OK because you told me it is' excuses that mean we torture and kill and do all the horrific activities imaginable. It's not OK, read books, scan newspapers, watch films and documentaries, don't imbibe the news. I think this is where Banks was coming from.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13453995928227613313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

It is not my stuff, and I'm disappointed past half way through to find it is a rather shallow thing and that Banks, with all his talent, wit and affectionate humour, is neither interesting, novel nor funny in this travesty of talent that is clearly a commercial thing aimed at the young adult market and American, most prob</span>
  <span id="freeText1516012257152658117" style="display:none">"Everybody plays the same game; it's just some people make more money out of their version." (Abacus 1990-2009 version, p138).<br /><br />This about sums up the facile nature of this book. It is not my stuff, and I'm disappointed past half way through to find it is a rather shallow thing and that Banks, with all his talent, wit and affectionate humour, is neither interesting, novel nor funny in this travesty of talent that is clearly a commercial thing aimed at the young adult market and American, most probably, at that too ('gotten').<br /><br />His writing here flows, as usual, but it is mediocre, teenie and slightly gutter. It had better have a good twist to redeem it, because there isn't a character that's rounded, remotely entertaining or worth saving - in plot or in print. Less the first person than the last you want to read of his, I'd say. Sold on reputation, I'd say, also.<br /><br />And then - albeit a wee too late and several dull chapters too far - the last section almost redeems the entire book, if not the concept; for the concept is immature, boorish and boring. Who wants to read about a plastic life? But we finally return to a familiar Banks in pleasantly ruminative mode, a breath of fresh air in a book devoted to stale old ideas, even though well articulated. Had I known...</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17333768381015896732">I loved this novel. As with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a>, I have my younger brother to thank. We were both keen musicians in our day, and he was a bassist - as is the protagonist of this story.<br/><br/>It's a first-person recollection by the bassist in a successful supergroup. He was the driving creative person, but always felt a little alien because he wasn't part of the original line-up, and came from a poor working-class background; whereas they're all middle-class. When we meet Daniel Weir he's living as a reclu</span>
  <span id="freeText17333768381015896732" style="display:none">I loved this novel. As with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a>, I have my younger brother to thank. We were both keen musicians in our day, and he was a bassist - as is the protagonist of this story.<br /><br />It's a first-person recollection by the bassist in a successful supergroup. He was the driving creative person, but always felt a little alien because he wasn't part of the original line-up, and came from a poor working-class background; whereas they're all middle-class. When we meet Daniel Weir he's living as a recluse in a giant folly of a building in Glasgow. Although the band is modelled on something like Fleetwood Mac, Dan Weir takes after Fish from Marillion.<br /><br />It's a cracking read, and much more mainstream than just about any Banks' work - this in spite of the fact that it's about such an odd life. It's not a heartbreaking story, but you'll just love the yarns, and they are all believable. For example, Ozzy Osbourne's lead guitarist was killed during an insane light-aircraft stunt in the early Eighties. You will laugh deep and plenty.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2358434823573731313">Iain Banks is simply my favourite Scottish author. From the first novel to the last I loved his books. His death in June of this year was a tragic loss to Scottish writing but he left a wonderful legacy. So I can’t envisage my country in books without an Iain Banks novel. <br/><br/>I picked Espedair Street for three reasons – firstly Daniel Weir the main character is the most fantastic of anti-heroes, secondly it’s a brilliant tale of sex, drugs, rock and roll so what’s not to love about it and thirdly I </span>
  <span id="freeText2358434823573731313" style="display:none">Iain Banks is simply my favourite Scottish author. From the first novel to the last I loved his books. His death in June of this year was a tragic loss to Scottish writing but he left a wonderful legacy. So I can’t envisage my country in books without an Iain Banks novel. <br /><br />I picked Espedair Street for three reasons – firstly Daniel Weir the main character is the most fantastic of anti-heroes, secondly it’s a brilliant tale of sex, drugs, rock and roll so what’s not to love about it and thirdly I used to live near Espedair Street in Paisley!! <br /><br />We have a word in Glasgow for something that’s wonderous in every way – we call it “gallus”. However, being Glaswegians, we can add to that to make it EVEN MORE wonderous in every way – because in the way the rest of the world uses “very”, in Glasgow we use “f***in”! Iain Banks and all his books, but especially Espedair Street, are f***in gallus!<br /><br />I chose Iain Banks Espedair Street as one of ten books that represent my country, Scotland. If you are interested in reading about the others you can find them here at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://theonlywayisreading.com/2013/12/18/no-sign-of-mel-gibson-ten-books-that-represent-my-country/">http://theonlywayisreading.com/2013/1...</a></span>

    <a title="Clare Neilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11358214-clare-neilson"><img alt="Clare Neilson" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/989823978?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clare Neilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clare Neilson" href="/user/show/11358214-clare-neilson">Clare Neilson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989823978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6860321526765081447">Yet another solid classic from Banksy. It's so hard to write a book about music no one has ever heard, but this comes very, very close to getting the songs in your head without ever hearing a note. The mood of the book goes from grim to elation and down again steeply. Banksy has a wicked way with writing character deaths and there's a couple in here you wouldn't believe would be possible, but everything is set up just so, making them as real as any others.<br/><br/>A rock "legend" in hiding the lead chara</span>
  <span id="freeText6860321526765081447" style="display:none">Yet another solid classic from Banksy. It's so hard to write a book about music no one has ever heard, but this comes very, very close to getting the songs in your head without ever hearing a note. The mood of the book goes from grim to elation and down again steeply. Banksy has a wicked way with writing character deaths and there's a couple in here you wouldn't believe would be possible, but everything is set up just so, making them as real as any others.<br /><br />A rock "legend" in hiding the lead character is the typical every-man who, some would say, got lucky. Told entirely from his point of view, however, we quickly learn of his background in a tiny flat with numerous siblings, and it becomes harder to feel it was simply luck that lead him down the path to stardom. The golden triptych of sex, drugs and rock 'n' roll are all here in the form of early love, whipped cream canister gas and learning to play bass instead of guitar because your fingers are too big. If you've ever enjoyed reading a Q magazine from cover to cover, you'll love this book.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6860321526765081447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2289156011" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2289156011" />
    <a title="Andy Cunningham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60324391-andy-cunningham"><img alt="Andy Cunningham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475778113p2/60324391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2289156011?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy Cunningham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy Cunningham" href="/user/show/60324391-andy-cunningham">Andy Cunningham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2289156011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13318832095375730356">Would we have had Irvine Welsh without Iain Banks? Written 6 years before Trainspotting, Espedair Street comes across like the big brother in the family. When Dan Weir, the main character, appears before us in his unkempt, unwashed, awkward, clumsy, misshapen manner, the last thing I was expecting was the story of a 70's rock star coming to terms with the end of a stellar career. But that's what it's about. In fact, the first half of the book feels like a fictional version of a rock stars memoir</span>
  <span id="freeText13318832095375730356" style="display:none">Would we have had Irvine Welsh without Iain Banks? Written 6 years before Trainspotting, Espedair Street comes across like the big brother in the family. When Dan Weir, the main character, appears before us in his unkempt, unwashed, awkward, clumsy, misshapen manner, the last thing I was expecting was the story of a 70's rock star coming to terms with the end of a stellar career. But that's what it's about. In fact, the first half of the book feels like a fictional version of a rock stars memoirs. Banks carries the mood off perfectly though, as the second half kicks into gear, while developing a character that has flaws, but also one who cares for those around him while also recognising his own shortcomings. That he doesn't see the same in others just makes us love him all the more. There are some hilarious set pieces that feel like Renton, Begbie and the boys would have been doing as a 5 piece rock band conquering the charts and earning millions touring the world. So much so that I can't believe there isn't a film of this. Come on Danny Boyle, where are you??!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13318832095375730356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2289156011">

<div id="review_665107666" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/665107666" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/556887-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/665107666?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/556887-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer665107666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3829732359413438225">This didn't quite work for me-- I never really believed in Danny Weir, and the kind of music his band makes-- hooky prog?-- doesn't really interest me. <br/><br/>The structure of the book see-saws back and forth, a chapter in the present, where Weird is retired and a little aimless, and then a chapter in the past, tracing the development of the band. Those past chapters mostly focus around Weird relationship, or at least adventures with another member of the band, which is kind of cool, but maybe isn't pu</span>
  <span id="freeText3829732359413438225" style="display:none">This didn't quite work for me-- I never really believed in Danny Weir, and the kind of music his band makes-- hooky prog?-- doesn't really interest me. <br /><br />The structure of the book see-saws back and forth, a chapter in the present, where Weird is retired and a little aimless, and then a chapter in the past, tracing the development of the band. Those past chapters mostly focus around Weird relationship, or at least adventures with another member of the band, which is kind of cool, but maybe isn't pushed far enough to really work for me.<br /><br />I wanted to like this, and I have a really cool Futura paperback edition with an awesome mandala cover. But I didn't think this was fresh, bracing, or challenging enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3829732359413438225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1705292205?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Louise Atkin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Louise Atkin" href="/user/show/15238324-rachel-louise-atkin">Rachel Louise Atkin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1705292205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13801201032466771074">There is nothing I can fault about Banks' prose. The characters and voices that he creates are always so amazing and interesting, and it's something I'd love to replicate in my own writing.<br/>This is about an old rockstar who's going through a midlife crisis, and we follow him as he reminisces on his life in the band and on tour. It's awesome and funny, and at times thought provoking.<br/>I didn't love this as much as The Wasp Factory because the story wasn't as insane, but Banks is someone I really wan</span>
  <span id="freeText13801201032466771074" style="display:none">There is nothing I can fault about Banks' prose. The characters and voices that he creates are always so amazing and interesting, and it's something I'd love to replicate in my own writing.<br />This is about an old rockstar who's going through a midlife crisis, and we follow him as he reminisces on his life in the band and on tour. It's awesome and funny, and at times thought provoking.<br />I didn't love this as much as The Wasp Factory because the story wasn't as insane, but Banks is someone I really want to read more of.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2297139399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13380885352007344591">First-person sulking by an ambivalently Scottish, ambivalently Left, ambivalently alive Standard Banks Man. Book aims to study spiritual clumsiness and pop music, ending up in a mid-life crisis at 30. <br/><br/>Has its moments (“<i>We put a value on what we treasure, and so cheapen it</i>”; this line always gives me goosebumps “<i>her blonde hair slid across the pillow like gold chains over snow (and for an instant I thought </i>Suzanne takes you down…)”). <br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1702009155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13128411785617590564">I'm so glad that when I asked for recommendations on a good "first" Iain Banks novel to read, I had forgotten that I had read (and didn't enjoy) Wasp Factory. If I had remembered, I would have missed out on this wonderful book. From the start I absolutely loved the voice. The story was funny and sad and I truly cared about Weird's story. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2683087321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2683087321" />
    <a title="Bobbi Steele" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39858798-bobbi-steele"><img alt="Bobbi Steele" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570580736p2/39858798.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2683087321?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bobbi Steele" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bobbi Steele" href="/user/show/39858798-bobbi-steele">Bobbi Steele</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2683087321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17973585524346181852">There are few novels as sexist and self-centred as this one. The worst part? It feels like the author is projecting himself in this book. Thankfully, I live in Germany. Wouldn’t want him to see that all women aren’t mothers and sex objects made to please him and his giant ego. Wrote your own songs? It sounds more like you just learned how to write rhymed verse. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mHOtlMDlHEPi3hjKdM8zpTmHzlUQkfRvptjiRynjoLPp9MuHXuUasXHryIJvISFksTIrTkWU1P+IEYZKiEW2Eg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kPMSzBAW43ZCrY+4SnsMdoaYcrlKZ3u2bUWCe7HLTKXhdHTfjhblhNGYX/BRlR63Di2Xoh9iWyZDjOZ2EG1aBA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7628.Iain_Banks">About Iain Banks</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7628.Iain_Banks"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1374456581p3/7628.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7628.Iain_Banks">Iain Banks
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,399
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17219034709236483775">This author also published science fiction under the pseudonym <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M__Banks' title='Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Iain M. Banks</a>.<br/><br/>Banks's father was an officer in the Admiralty and his mother was once a professional ice skater. Iain Banks was educated at the University of Stirling where he studied English Literature, Philosophy and Psychology. He moved to London and lived in the south of England until 1988 when he returned to Scotland, living in Edi</span>
  <span id="freeText17219034709236483775" style="display:none">This author also published science fiction under the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M__Banks" title="Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Iain M. Banks</a>.<br /><br />Banks's father was an officer in the Admiralty and his mother was once a professional ice skater. Iain Banks was educated at the University of Stirling where he studied English Literature, Philosophy and Psychology. He moved to London and lived in the south of England until 1988 when he returned to Scotland, living in Edinburgh and then Fife.<br /><br />Banks met his wife Annie in London, before the release of his first book. They married in Hawaii in 1982. However, he announced in early 2007 that, after 25 years together, they had separated. He lived most recently in North Queensferry, a town on the north side of the Firth of Forth near the Forth Bridge and the Forth Road Bridge.<br /><br />As with his friend Ken MacLeod (another Scottish writer of technical and social science fiction) a strong awareness of left-wing history shows in his writings. The argument that an economy of abundance renders anarchy and adhocracy viable (or even inevitable) attracts many as an interesting potential experiment, were it ever to become testable. He was a signatory to the Declaration of Calton Hill, which calls for Scottish independence.<br /><br />In late 2004, Banks was a prominent member of a group of British politicians and media figures who campaigned to have Prime Minister Tony Blair impeached following the 2003 invasion of Iraq. In protest he cut up his passport and posted it to 10 Downing Street. In an interview in Socialist Review he claimed he did this after he "abandoned the idea of crashing my Land Rover through the gates of Fife dockyard, after spotting the guys armed with machine guns." He related his concerns about the invasion of Iraq in his book <i>Raw Spirit</i>, and the principal protagonist (Alban McGill) in the novel <i>The Steep Approach to Garbadale</i> confronts another character with arguments in a similar vein.<br /><br />Interviewed on Mark Lawson's BBC Four series, first broadcast in the UK on 14 November 2006, Banks explained why his novels are published under two different names. His parents wished to name him Iain Menzies Banks but his father made a mistake when registering the birth and he was officially registered as Iain Banks. Despite this he continued to use his unofficial middle name and it was as Iain M. Banks that he submitted <i>The Wasp Factory</i> for publication. However, his editor asked if he would mind dropping the 'M' as it appeared "too fussy". The editor was also concerned about possible confusion with Rosie M. Banks, a minor character in some of P.G. Wodehouse's Jeeves novels who is a romantic novelist. After his first three mainstream novels his publishers agreed to publish his first SF novel, <i>Consider Phlebas</i>. To distinguish between the mainstream and SF novels, Banks suggested the return of the 'M', although at one stage he considered John B. Macallan as his SF pseudonym, the name deriving from his favourite whiskies: Johnnie Walker Black Label and The Macallan single malt.<br /><br />His latest book was a science fiction (SF) novel in the Culture series, called <i>The Hydrogen Sonata</i>, published in 2012.<br /><br />Author Iain M. Banks revealed in April 2013 that he had late-stage cancer. He died the following June.<br /><br />The Scottish writer posted a message on his official website saying his next novel <i>The Quarry</i>, due to be published later this year*, would be his last.<br /><br />* <i>The Quarry</i> was published in June 2013.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17219034709236483775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
